AVERROIS IN ARISTOTELEM

PREFACE

This is the fifth volume in a series of critical editions of the Arabic text of Averroes's Middle Commentaries on Aristotle's logical works. The four other volumes originally intended to precede this one, but which for reasons discussed below will be published subsequently, contain critical editions of the Arabic text of Averroes's Middle Commentaries on Aristotle's Categories, De Interpretatione, Prior Analytics, and Posterior Analytics. Although the fifth of Averroes's Middle Commentaries on Aristotle's logical works, this volume is numbered as the sixth because Averroes's Middle Commentary on Porphyry's Isagoge, which to our knowledge has not survived in the Arabic original, represents the introduction to these works and is designated as the first volume of the series. The Hebrew version of that work has survived and has been edited as the first volume. Publication of these Arabic texts has been undertaken in order to complete and extend the ambitious project begun by Professor Mahmoud Kassem a few years before his death. Thus their publication is meant to stand as a scholarly testimonial to the esteem and affection with which he is remembered by students and colleagues throughout the world.

Were it not for a series of coincidences, this volume would have been published in keeping with the traditional order of Averroes's Middle Commentaries on Aristotle's logical works. Shortly after it was decided to complete the work Professor Kassem had begun on the first four of these Middle Commentaries, a number of previously ignored manuscripts came to light. Thanks largely to the good will of colleagues and scholarly associates who shared discoveries they had made about improperly identified and incorrectly catalogued manuscripts of these texts, as well as to painstaking examination of bibliographical journals and the microfilm listings of the Arab League's Institute of Arabic Manuscripts in Cairo, nine new manuscripts were discovered. Unfortunately, most of these manuscripts are located in distant libraries whose collections are not readily accessible. Thus, while collating some of these manuscripts and waiting for

copies of the others, it seemed appropriate to go ahead with the edition of the Arabic text of Averroes's Middle Commentary on Aristotle's Topics— a text extant only in two easily accessible manuscripts. For these reasons, what was to appear later now appears first. Hopefully, it will be possible to finish the Arabic editions of the other treatises shortly.

It is a pleasure to acknowledge the persons and institutions who have contributed so generously to the appearance of this volume. Above all, I am grateful to Professor Muhsin Mahdi for his encouragement, assistance, and critical advice at every stage of this project. If any one individual can be singled out as the guiding force in the renaissance of the study of Islamic philosophy, it is he. The administrators of the Fulbright-Hays Faculty Research Fellowship program also deserve special recognition for their graciousness and understanding in allowing me to alter my research plans completely and to abandon one project in order to begin this one. The primary sponsors of this project, otherwise known as the Project in Medieval Islamic Logic, have been the American Research Center in Egypt and the Smithsonian Institution. While everyone connected with these institutions has been most helpful, Paul E. Walker, Director of the American Research Center in Egypt, deserves a special word of appreciation. The American Philosophical Society has also sponsored part of the research connected with this project. I also wish to thank Hammam Fauzi Hassan for his important assistance at the beginning stages of this edition. Finally, I wish to express my sincere gratitude to Ahmad Abd al-Magid Haridi, who has contributed his rich knowledge of Arabic grammar and his long experience with manuscripts to this subject matter otherwise foreign to his area of expertise and who has been ever willing to adapt his skills to newer standards of critical editorship. In sum, he has been a most worthy co-worker.

C.E.B
CAIRO
September, 1978

EDITOR'S INTRODUCTION

The Teaching of the Text

The Edition

Conclusion of this part of the book (para. 367)249
Index251
I

For Aristotle, dialectic is fundamentally different from the art of demonstration. He considers it so important to distinguish the one from the other that he opens his treatise on dialectic with a detailed enumeration of their differences. Whereas demonstration reasons from premises which are true and primary or at least themselves based on such premises, dialectic reasons from premises based on generally accepted opinions. However, this distinction should not be understood as an indication that dialectic has no value for intellectual investigation. To the contrary, Aristotle's explanation of the uses to which dialectic may be put clearly illustrates his favorable appreciation of its investigative merit in both the practical and the theoretical arts and sciences.(1) Insofar as dialectic provides mental training or exercise, those who use it correctly learn to look at both sides of questions and to argue about their own positions. Moreover, its usefulness for conversing with the populace — a usefulness grounded on dialectic's reliance on generally accepted opinions — consists in allowing the dialectician to pursue arguments with the people according to their own terms and to avoid introducing his terms; as a result he can more easily discuss the popular understanding of the practical arts and sciences. Finally, dialectic is helpful in the pursuit of the philosophical sciences. Besides allowing its practitioner to raise questions on both sides of an issue in order to advance the discussion, it is useful for getting at the first principles of these sciences.

Despite the significance of this last feature of dialectic's usefulness as an investigative tool, it is clear that Aristotle does not consider apprehension of the first principles to be the end of learning. For him, it is only the beginning. Once the first principles have been secured, the philosopher can inquire into the particulars of each science by means of demonstrative reasoning. In this sense, Aristotle's appreciation of the art is vastly different from that of Socrates — or at least of Plato's Socrates — as we know his views from the Republic. In this work, dialectic is presented as the art which permits the philosopher

to see the good, i.e., to know the truth about the whole. Starting from generally received opinions, characterized by Socrates as shadows and distortions, of what is, the thoughtful man uses dialectic to ascend by means of question and answer to an apprehension of the truth of things — that is, to the idea or form of things and ultimately to the idea of the good. According to this description of dialectic, no provision is made for stopping at the idea of a particular science and for changing vehicles so that the particulars of the science may be apprehended. To the contrary, such a description presents dialectic as passing through the particulars in order to arrive at a comprehension of the unity which lies behind them.(2)

Averroes, that indefatigable commentator of Aristotle, could not have been unaware of this difference of opinion between Plato and Aristotle concerning dialectic. After all, he was familiar enough with the Republic to write a commentary on it — a commentary, moreover, from which he claimed to have carefully exorcised all dialectical arguments.(3) Whether Averroes actually fulfilled this claim or not, it is clear that his attempt to do so enabled him to refrain from any reference to the investigative merits of dialectic propounded by Socrates. Asserting that the sciences had been completed by his time and that there was consequently no reason to use anything but demonstrative arguments, he sought to bar dialectical arguments from his commentary. Such a procedure indicates that Averroes did not share Plato's or Socrates's opinion about the investigative merits of dialectic and that he was inclined to approach Plato's work from something of an Aristotelian perspective. The latter tendency becomes all the more evident when it is noted that he replaced the whole context surrounding the discussion of the divided line with an account of human perfection taken from several Aristotelian works and modified the presentation of the education of the philosophers, which culminates in a firm grasp of dialectic as the investigative tool par excellence, by omitting any discussion of dialectic and embarking instead on a digression about the proper place for the study of logic in such an education.(4) Each of these modifications permitted him to mute the importance of dialectic.

Still, it is Averroes's Middle Commentary on Aristotle's Topics, not his Commentary on Plato's Republic,(5) which interests us here. It is worth calling attention to the latter work only to emphasize Averroes's

awareness of the basic disagreement between Plato and Aristotle concerning the merits of dialectic and to suggest that his Middle Commentary on Aristotle's Topics is a perfect counterpart to his Commentary on Plato's Republic. In this sense it would not be too much of an exaggeration to say that Averroes approaches Aristotle's work from a Platonic perspective. Indeed, his account of what Aristotle has to say about the art of dialectic results in the portrait of an art far closer to demonstration than to sophistry or rhetoric. And in that respect he makes Aristotle appear to be in substantial agreement with Plato concerning dialectic.

II

Averroes manages to bring these two great protagonists together without directly alluding to the issues that divide them. Instead, taking no great pains to distinguish between what Aristotle actually says and what he himself deems appropriate, Averroes gives Aristotle's teaching a new twist. For example, not content simply to explain how Aristotle distinguishes dialectic from sophistry, Averroes increases the force of Aristotle's statements as well as the number of references to the differences between the two arts. To be sure, he repeats Aristotle's explanation of how some arguments are based on opinions which only seem to be generally received or only appear to be based on generally received opinions and, like Aristotle, he insists that this kind of argument is foreign to dialectic. Averroes also repeats Aristotle's strictures against the use of contentious procedures in dialectic and his suggestions that they can be admitted only when speaking with those who themselves use such procedures. Yet in each of these instances, Averroes's language is far stronger than Aristotle's: he restricts the legitimate use of sophistical procedures in dialectic much more than Aristotle and goes far beyond him in his unflattering description of those who normally use such arguments. Above all, not content with commenting on the seven passages in which Aristotle attempts to distinguish dialectic from sophistry, Averroes returns to the same subject three additional times. He does so even though in at least two of those instances his remarks have no parallel whatever in Aristotle's text.(6)

Clearly, it would not be possible to recognize these kinds of differences without carefully comparing Averroes's text to Aristotle's.

Such a comparative reading also reveals other liberties that Averroes takes with Aristotle's text. Thus, without alerting the reader in any fashion, Averroes rearranges different passages in Aristotle's text, fails to comment on large portions of it, and introduces his own examples in place of Aristotle's. In addition, he frequently omits Aristotle's examples without explanation or substitution.

The comparative reading which reveals such differences between Averroes's text and Aristotle's is prompted less by a desire to see how faithfully Averroes follows Aristotle than by the curiosity Averroes arouses through occasional remarks about the procedure he follows in this commentary. For example, at the end of the first major part of the commentary, he says:

This concludes the discussion of the first portion of this science, and most of what we have mentioned about it is everything that is in the first book of Aristotle's treatise on dialectic.

In other words, Averroes emphasizes that some of what he has said in this part of the text has nothing to do with Aristotle's account. Such a statement accords very well with the way he opens his treatise by giving a general explanation of dialectic that centers around his own concerns rather than around Aristotle's and that acknowledges its ultimate dependence on Aristotle only as a kind of afterthought. Similarly, at the end of the third and final major part of the commentary, Averroes declares:

We have fashioned this statement about all of the necessary ideas contained in the final book of the treatise in as succinct and as clear a manner as possible.

Surely, such a declaration prompts the reader to wonder about the ideas Averroes deems to be non-necessary.

In the second major part of his commentary, the part devoted to a discussion of the topics occurring in Books II-VII of Aristotle's treatise, Averroes refers repeatedly to the Greek commentators and explicitly criticizes the way Aristotle handles certain subjects. He turns to Theophrastus, Themistius, and Alexander because he considers their presentation of these topics to be more orderly and comprehensible than Aristotle's. He also turns to these commentators, as well

as to al-Fārābī, at the beginning of his account of these topics in order to explain how the term "topic" should be understood, for such a discussion does not occur in Aristotle's text.7 In other passages his explicit criticisms of the dialectical theologians (mutakallimūn) and references to the mathematicians of his own time testify to the personal mark he wishes to place on this commentary.8

Now these remarks about the immediately apparent ways in which Averroes underlines his independence from Aristotle are not intended as suggestions that this work is anything other than a commentary. Far too much evidence to the contrary can be brought against such a thesis. For example, at the beginning of the treatise Averroes states that his discussion of dialectic will proceed in the form of a commentary on Aristotle's Topics. He also explains that he divides his treatise into three parts corresponding to the major divisions he discerns in Aristotle's work. Moreover, he closes each of these three parts with an acknowledgement that the preceding discussion was intended as an explanation of Aristotle's text, and he relates each part or its sub-sections to a particular book of the Topics.9 In addition, on at least sixty-nine different occasions—that is, about once every three pages, he explicitly mentions Aristotle's name and speaks about what Aristotle thought or did or tried to do in this work. And there are twenty-five other instances which can only be understood as references to Aristotle, even though no one is explicitly named. Accordingly, these remarks are intended as indications that in this Middle Commentary on Aristotle's Topics, as in most of his other Middle Commentaries on Aristotle's logical works, Averroes approaches Aristotle's work in a strikingly independent manner.

It is necessary to draw attention to the superficial features of Averroes's independent approach because heretofore so much has been made of the superficial features of his texts in order to perpetuate the notion that Averroes is slavishly faithful to Aristotle's thought and word. The proponents of this notion have insisted above all on Averroes's use of the verb qāl (he said) to indicate a direct quotation from Aristotle and have asserted that his texts are laden with such quotations. However, here, as in the other Middle Commentaries on Aristotle's logical works, these direct quotations occur very rarely. Although this commentary consists of about 50,000 words, Averroes uses the conventional sign for quotation no more than twenty-three times. Even if other forms of the word are

taken into account — e.g., yaqûl (he says) or qawlûh (his statement) only eight more instances can be adduced.(10) On the other hand, the verb naqûl (we say) and its derivatives are abundantly present. They occur far more frequently, sometimes as often as three or four times in a single line.

Apart from these immediately evident signs of Averroes's willingness to depart from Aristotle's text, a very simple fact must not be overlooked. Averroes is explicit about his differences with Aristotle only with respect to questions of form — i.e., the way certain topics are presented or arranged and Aristotle's failure to explain what he means by a topic. When questions of substance are involved, especially those centering around Averroes's attempts to bring Plato and Aristotle into closer agreement, he does not trumpet his innovations. They can be discerned only by recourse to the close, comparative reading referred to earlier.

III

As has been noted, Aristotle's treatise consists of eight books. In the first, Aristotle discusses the art of dialectic in general and explains how it functions as a logical art. The following six books are devoted to an exposition of the various general lines of argument or topics that can be used in dialectic and to a detailed explanation of the different ways in which they might be employed. Then, in the last book, Aristotle addresses a series of suggestions to both the questioner and the answerer with a view to helping them acquire a sound grasp of the elements of dialectical conversations, and he also explains the practical limitations of this art. Although fully aware of Aristotle's division of his treatise into eight books, Averroes prefers to divide his own commentary into three major parts. Thus, the first part is intended as a commentary on the first book of Aristotle's Topics, the long second part as a commentary on and analysis of the topics presented in the second through the seventh books, and the third part as a commentary on the eighth book.

Generally speaking, Aristotle's discussion in Book One of the Topics consists of three parts. The first chapter opens with a statement about the kind of reasoning appropriate to dialectic, and the next two chapters contain a discussion of what dialectic is useful for, as well as of what constitutes dialectical skill. Aristotle then turns to an enumeration of the things dialectical arguments are concerned

with and of what they consist. This prompts him to speak of the differences between dialectical propositions and problems, and to explain what these predicables have in common. Because he considers it necessary to state more precisely what they have in common, he distinguishes the different ways in which things are similar, shows how both induction and syllogism point to the common character of the predicables, and explains why each of the predicables applies to the various categories. Turning to a more detailed discussion of how dialectical arguments may be obtained, Aristotle again distinguishes between dialectical propositions and dialectical problems, as well as between these two groups and theses in general, and then explains the character of induction. This whole discussion centered around dialectical arguments, propositions, and problems corresponds to what may be called the second part of the first book.(11) In the last part, Aristotle provides a general account of how dialectical syllogisms may be obtained and then enters upon a detailed enumeration of four particular ways: securing propositions, distinguishing the senses in which a word or term is used, exploiting the difference in their use, and examining the similarities in their use. He concludes by speaking about the importance of these kinds of distinctions for the practice of dialectic.

At best, Averroes follows Aristotle's text loosely. While the title of the work leaves no doubt but what Averroes perceives his account of the art of dialectic to be intimately related to Aristotle's Topics,(12) the treatise opens on a very independent note. In the first paragraph [Averroes explains the goal of the work as that of making known the rules and general things which constitute the art of dialectic and explains in general how the art is used between two people. Any mention of the subject of Aristotle's first chapter — i. e., the reasoning appropriate to dialectic — is conspicuously absent from this first paragraph or from the other six paragraphs which comprise the introductory section of Averroes's commentary. Only at a much later stage of the argument does he introduce the distinction Aristotle considered to be prior to all other discussion.(13) After a remarkably free adaptation of some of Aristotle's remarks concerning the way dialectic is practiced, Averroes closes the first paragraph of his commentary with a highly questionable etymological explanation of why Aristotle labeled this art dialectic (jadal). As Averroes would have it, Aristotle chose to call the art by this name because it reflects an opin-

ion held by the multitude, an opinion which likens dialectical discussion to contentious argument.(14) The context of this highly fanciful etymological attribution suggests that Averroes wishes to align himself with Aristotle in order to warn his readers of the dangers which might befall those who fail to practice dialectic in accordance with the rules presented here.

In the rest of the introductory section, Averroes quietly extends Aristotle's remarks about the uses of dialectic so that it appears as an art with wide-ranging investigative possibilities, an art not easily discernible from demonstration or from philosophy. Thus, while he follows Aristotle's text to the extent of reporting that dialectic has three uses, he goes beyond Aristotle in the way he interprets each one. For example, a claim that dialectic is useful for training in the theoretical sciences and that this training aims at preparation for philosophy replaces Aristotle's suggestion that dialectic can be useful for training in argument. To support this claim, dialectical training as preparation for philosophy is likened to training in horsemanship as preparation for warfare.(15) For Averroes, then, both dialectic and horsemanship are in the service of higher arts.

Similarly, Aristotle's reflection about the usefulness of dialectic for conversing with the many are so many reasons for Averroes to vaunt its usefulness for instructing the multitude in what they need to believe for political association. Calling upon the reader's familiarity with the teaching presented in political science, he asserts the necessity for the citizens to agree about certain questions in order to associate in justice and virtue. His contention here is that such agreement can more firmly be established by means of dialectical than rhetorical or poetic arguments.(16) In setting forth this opinion, he says nothing about the way these different kinds of arguments are meant to appeal to the different intellectual capacities of the people. As such, the argument is at variance with Averroes's teaching elsewhere and clearly opposed to what his readers have learned about political science. Such a use of dialectic is appropriate only for those who already have a basic opinion about the different sciences but are not able to go beyond that opinion to demonstrative knowledge. Averroes ignores these distinctions purposely here in order to affirm the political usefulness of dialectic more emphatically.

Finally, Aristotle's explanation about the usefulness of dialectic

with respect to the philosophic sciences is completely transformed by Averroes. For example, he presents Aristotle's explanation of dialectic's usefulness for raising difficulties with respect to both sides of a question as a feature which permits the dialectician to distinguish essential premises from accidental premises and thereby arrive at the true understanding of a problem. To defend this novel presentation, he offers two arguments to the effect that the dialectician can use demonstrative tests to distinguish essential from accidental premises. First, he cites what actually occurs in the practical arts as an example of such a procedure. Then he refers to the practical science of politics and to theoretical sciences such as theology and physics, explaining that Aristotle started with dialectical arguments in each of these sciences and moved to demonstrative arguments afterwards.(17) Such an argument not only presents dialectic as an art in the service of philosophy or of demonstrative reasoning, it also emphasizes the possibility of acquiring demonstrative or theoretical knowledge about a practical science like politics, as well as about the theoretical sciences.

Averroes introduces another argument about the usefulness of dialectic with respect to the philosophical sciences which stresses once again its role as an art in the service of demonstration. He contends that dialectic is also useful insofar as it enables us to discern whether a proposition which has been set down as true is indeed so according to the rules of demonstration. Yet this argument is simply his own; it has no parallel in Aristotle's text.(18)

Still another instance of Averroes's willingness to bring dialectic into close association with demonstration occurs in his explanation of the way dialectic may be used to get at the first principles of the arts and sciences. Aristotle attributes this usefulness to dialectic because of his conviction that the first principles of any particular art or science cannot be apprehended according to the principles of the particular art or science and that it is therefore necessary to approach them according to generally received opinions.(19) Averroes goes beyond Aristotle's explanation here and suggests five different ways in which dialectic has such a usefulness. Above all, he attributes this usefulness to the role played in dialectic by the induction, but — promising to say more about the subject in the sequel — he does not explain here why the induction is so important. Instead, he places major emphasis on the generally received character of the opinions

used in dialectical propositions and illustrates this aspect of their usefulness by interpretations of Aristotle's procedure in other works. For example, contending that dialectic can be used to help the beginner in an art form a concept of the principles of that art and eventually assent to them, he urges the reader to reflect upon the way Aristotle presented his teaching in the De Interpretatione. Averroes also traces dialectic's usefulness to the way it permits the beginner in an art to go beyond the question-begging arguments he might encounter. Although this opinion is not mentioned by Aristotle in the present context, he does develop it later in the work.(20) By calling attention to it here, then, Averroes has simply made a more orderly presentation of Aristotle's argument. Averroes returns to his interpretation of Aristotle's use of dialectic in other works and enumerates two other ways in which dialectic can be useful for getting at the principles of the arts or sciences. Thus he cites Aristotle's refutations of certain sophists in the Physics as an example of how dialectic can be used to counter the arguments such people set forth about the principles of the sciences. He also claims that dialectic is especially useful with the kind of demonstrations that are used to establish what is self-evident against those who deny it and mentions as an example the kind of arguments used by Aristotle when discussing the partial sciences in the Metaphysics. Differently stated, Averroes contends that some of Aristotle's arguments about physics are dialectical. He says nothing about how dialectic might be useful with respect to the other kinds of demonstrative arguments — i. e., those concerning what is not known by nature.

When all of these instances of Averroes's departures from Aristotle's text are considered, it becomes evident that his commentary is more interpretative than imitative. While some of his explanations do show an attempt to make Aristotle's argument more readily understandable or an attempt to present the arguments in a more orderly manner, others contribute to what may best be called his own teaching about dialectic. For him, dialectic is a universal art which is primarily in the service of demonstration, but which also has important practical uses. Because a clearer understanding of theoretical questions can be acquired by dialectical arguments, i.e., arguments based on generally received opinions, it now appears to be more useful than the other non-demonstrative logical arts for communicating the essence of such questions to the citizens. For

Aristotle, dialectic is a universal art with its own independent sphere. It is no more in the service of demonstration than any other art. It is simply an art which permits its practitioner to reason according to sound logical rules and to use generally accepted opinions to formulate arguments or problems. As such, it can be used in discussions with the general body of citizens. But when it is used in this way, it is used to explore the significance of the soundness of the opinions held by the citizens, not to instruct them by presenting truths acquired from demonstrative reasoning in more acceptable language.

The same dual purpose is evident in the rest of the commentary. At times, Averroes is a faithful and highly solicitous commentator of Aristotle, a commentator who reflects upon the difference between Aristotle's teaching and the way that teaching corresponds to linguistic conventions in Arabic or who brings disparate parts of Aristotle's teaching together so that the reader might more readily grasp it. At other times, however, he pursues his own view of the art and adapts Aristotle's teaching to his needs without alerting the reader. Instances of this procedure abound in the second section (I, paras. 8-27) of this first part of Averroes's commentary. In addition to two other attempts to present dialectic as a quasi-philosophic art, Averroes also modifies Aristotle's arguments about the four predicables common to dialectical propositions and introduces a number of examples that extend Aristotle's teaching in a different direction.

Whereas Aristotle first identifies three predicables — definition, property, and genus — and then introduces the fourth—accident—after explaining that he intends to bring differentia under genus in this work, Averroes first identifies six predicables — definition, genus, differentia, property, general description (rasm), and accident — and then eliminates both differentia and general description. This whole explanation takes on significance because the predicable of general description derives from Porphyry's introduction to Aristotle's logic, a work Averroes previously rejected as being of no help for understanding Aristotle's logical treatises. As he explained at the time, he would have preferred not to preface this series of commentaries with an account of Porphyry's Isagoge and eventually did so only because friends had beseeched him to explain that work. He was fully persuaded that the argument of the Isagoge was more

helpful to a student pursuing Aristotle's teaching in the Posterior Analytics or in the Topics — i.e., to a student already familiar with the rudiments of Aristotle's logic — than to a student just embarking on the study of the Categories.(21) The comments he sets forth here about the predicables ought then to be understood as illustrative of how Averroes would like to use Porphyry's text to clarify Aristotle's teaching about dialectic.

Later, when Aristotle explains that the determination of one line of inquiry common to these four predicables is a difficult question whose resolution does not belong to a treatise on dialectic, Averroes agrees and then goes on to identify the Metaphysics as the treatise which resolves the question.(22) He has no more hesitation about explaining how other treatises of Aristotle contribute to the teaching set forth here than to calling upon the works of other authors to accomplish the same goal. In this sense, he seems to have no desire to preserve Aristotle's silences or to gloss over what might be considered as intentional errors. For example, Averroes takes great pains to offer an account of "oneness" and "other" which is more orderly than Aristotle's account. He draws out all the possible senses in which the notion of "one" can be used and offers examples of each; he collapses Aristotle's distinction between specific and generic sameness by introducing the notion of predicate sameness; and he presents Aristotle's remarks concerning accidental sameness in a manner that allows the reader to understand them as referring to relation. The immediate result of all these changes is a better understanding of Aristotle's remarks.

But that is not all. Averroes's procedure also allows him to introduce a number of examples that go beyond the immediate subject matter and that are without parallel in Aristotle's text. Among them is an example likening the rulership exercised by the Roman emperor to that exercised by the khalif in Islam. He follows this same procedure in the immediate sequel and brings forth a series of examples that suggest the presence of a hierarchy among the different kinds of generally received opinions, a hierarchy in which the opinions of the learned stand out as the most trustworthy.(23)

Although nothing can be found in Aristotle's text to contradict either one of these suggestions, there is also nothing to prompt them. Rather, each must be understood in the larger context

of Averroes's view of dialectic. He points to the similarity between kinds of rule and explains that a dialectical argument allows one to become aware of this similarity because of his desire to emphasize the political uses to which dialectic might be put. And he establishes a hierarchy that points to the trustworthiness of the opinions of the learned in order to further his own interpretation of the close relationship between dialectic and philosophy or, using the terms as interchangeably as he does, between dialectic and demonstration. In keeping with such goals, he turns from these illustrations to an enumeration of what ought and of what ought not to be done in the practice of dialectic. This enumeration is prefaced by the assertion that "the primary intent with respect to this art is that it be employed in the service of philosophy or to benefit the multitude" and should therefore be used in practical philosophy, theoretical philosophy, or in "that which serves as a tool to knowledge of these two arts — namely, logic."(24) As an example of how dialectic ought not to be used with respect to the theoretical sciences, Averroes cites two arguments which he attributes to the dialectical theologians. Although he does not say as much, each of the other two examples of how dialectic ought not to be used can also be traced to their teachings. By contrast, two of the examples concerning how this art ought to be used are set forth in his own name. And Averroes does well to speak in his own name here, for none of this has a parallel in Aristotle's text.

The opinions Averroes sets forth here about the merits of dialectic for the practical or theoretical arts and about the hierarchical status of the opinions of the learned do, however, have a parallel in Plato's Republic. In fact, Averroes's presentation of the art of dialectic thus far closely resembles the art praised by Socrates in the Republic and the Gorgias. Nonetheless, something is lacking here. Averroes has not yet explained what warrants bringing dialectic into such close association with demonstration. Differently stated, he has not yet shown why his remarks here should be given greater credence than the harsh ones he makes against dialectic in his commentary on the Republic. The exposition of the next few paragraphs responds precisely to this need, for it traces the close relationship between dialectic and demonstration to the quality of the arguments used in each art. Thus Averroes presents the dialectical syllogism as something that can be defined in terms of the

definition of the syllogism set forth in Aristotle's Prior Analytics. He refers the reader to that account of the syllogism and says nothing about the fact that this is the very definition used by Aristotle at the beginning of the Topics.(25) According to Averroes, the syllogism used in demonstration, in dialectic, and in most sophistical arguments differs only according to its matter — i.e., according to its premises. Otherwise, all three arts are alike insofar as they use a syllogism which is sound with respect to its form. The implication is that the syllogism used in these three arts differs from the one used in rhetoric because the rhetorical syllogism or enthymeme is usually lacking one premise and is therefore not sound with respect to its form. And it differs from the speeches used in poetics because they are not truly syllogistic. By insisting on the trustworthiness of the opinions of the learned, Averroes also minimizes the differences between the syllogism used in demonstration and that used in dialectic. His highly original attempts in the second part of this commentary to single out those topics which are demonstrative in character or to explain how they can be made demonstrative serve the same purpose.(26)

His explanation of the other kind of argument used in dialectic — the induction — is not directed to such a goal. Averroes defines the induction in much the same way as Aristotle: both agree that it is a form of reasoning which leads to a universal judgment about something on the basis of noting what is common to most or to all of its parts. As an example, Averroes cites the way awareness that a skilled artisan does his job better than an unskilled one or that a skilled horseman and a skilled carpenter practice their arts better than those lacking the appropriate skills leads to the conclusion that a skilled pilot will function better than an unskilled one. At the same time, however, he draws attention to the limits of dialectical induction. As used in this art, induction leads to universal judgments only on the basis of unexamined opinion and does so only accidentally. It is therefore radically different from the induction used in demonstration. That kind of induction is said to be such as to lead to essential certainty. Referring the reader to the discussion of this finer kind of induction in the Posterior Analytics, Averroes remains utterly silent here about the possibility of using dialectical induction to get at the first-principles of an art. To his way of thinking, its primary function

is to verify the universal premise used in the dialectical syllogism.(27)

He subordinates the induction to the syllogism because he thinks the syllogism is a more noble kind of reasoning. That conviction leads him to introduce his own explanation of how reasoning by analogy and comparative examples may be used to identify the different kinds of generally accepted opinions or to distinguish opinions about ethical and physical questions from those about logical questions. It is offered in place of Aristotle's explanation of the necessity of using induction to obtain knowledge about the different kinds of dialectical propositions and problems. The substitution also permits him to remain silent about the distinction Aristotle draws between philosophy and dialectic with respect to their ends. Thus, whereas Aristotle understands the two arts to differ insofar as philosophy aims at truth and dialectic at opinion, Averroes is content to leave the end aimed at in dialectic unspecified. His silence about the precise end of dialectic allows him to adduce additional parallels between the two arts and thereby to strengthen the impression that dialectic is in the service of philosophy or demonstration. And since his attempt to bring dialectic closer to demonstration is based primarily on the importance he ascribes to the dialectical syllogism, he frequently concentrates on it to such an extent that he neglects the dialectical induction.

A final instance of this kind of procedure in the first part of his commentary is worth citing. When Aristotle discusses the way the discovery of similarity can be used in dialectic, he mentions its merit for inductions, for hypothetical syllogisms, and for assigning definitions. He considers it useful for induction because it allows the dialectician to infer the universal from a series of particular similarities. Averroes says nothing at all about this argument. Instead, he concentrates on how knowledge of similarities allows the dialectician to pursue the essential meanings that occur in dialectical syllogisms.(28) In a word, his major concern is to explain how dialectic can be used to acquire demonstrative knowledge.

IV

That concern is prevalent in the second part of his commentary as well, but there it is subordinated to his attempt to present Aristotle's discussion of the dialectical topics in a coherent manner. As has been noted, Averroes has no hesitations about calling on other commentators to help him recognize the basic order of Aristotle's argument. Nor does he hesitate to suggest what he considers to be the force of different topics for the various kinds of logical arguments. Averroes is tempted to speak about the different topics in such terms, even though Aristotle never does so, because he understands the topic to be a premise used in constructing a syllogism or at least to be a constitutive element in a major, particular premise. This is an interpretation of the topic which he ascribes to Alexander and to al-Farabi, an interpretation which Averroes defends at length in the opening pages of the second part of his commentary. (29) And it is an interpretation which ought to affect in no way the appreciation he might form of the dialectical induction. If the dialectician is able to arrive at a major, particular premise for a dialectical syllogism by means of the topic, he ought to be able to arrive at a major, universal premise for such a syllogism by means of the induction. After all, the induction is what allows one to move from judgments about a series of particular phenomena to a general statement or judgment. Also, at least according to what Averroes says in the first part of the commentary, it is that which allows the dialectician to establish or verify the universal premise used in dialectical syllogisms.

Yet such an appreciation of the role of dialectical induction is conspicuously absent from this part of his commentary. Here Averroes assigns it only two uses. After explaining what he thinks the topic to be and discussing how Aristotle seeks to instruct the reader about these different topics, as well as suggesting other ways he might have so instructed the reader, Averroes concludes this lengthy introduction with an argument about why the topics are finite in number. At the end of that argument, he declares that he will

bring forth all of the different topics by means of induction. (30) In other words, Averroes deems induction useful for enumerating the particular topics. In the course of this inductive enumeration of the topics, he identifies another possible use for induction: it can be used to enumerate the particulars that allow some topics to be established or verified. Although this second use of the induction is one that Aristotle also mentions, Averroes discusses the instances in which induction might be put to such a use in a very different way than Aristotle. With one exception, he either concludes his discussion of such topics by explaining why an inductive verification of a particular topic makes it unsuitable as a premise of a demonstrative argument or by declaring that such a procedure is more suited to sophistry than to dialectic. (31) For the most part, however, Averroes simply remains silent about the uses Aristotle attributes to induction. In the discussion of one topic, he even goes so far as to substitute an enumeration of the different kinds of syllogisms with which this kind of topic might be used for Aristotle's explanation of how different inductive arguments might be used to verify the topic. (32)

Averroes's reluctance to give broad scope to the induction here seems to arise from a conviction that arguments based on induction are usually misleading. This apprehension becomes quite evident in his account of a topic concerning genus which is as important as it is involved and tendentious. The topic centers on that old Socratic assertion that virtue is knowledge, i.e., that virtue can be subsumed under the genus of knowledge. Aristotle approaches this question by looking at justice as one instance of virtue and denying that it can be subsumed under knowledge. What he means is that practical or moral virtue does not have knowledge as its genus. But such an argument raises the very difficult question of identifying the genus to which prudence, i.e., practical wisdom, belongs. Obviously thinking of Socrates, Aristotle acknowledges that some people consider prudence to be both virtue and knowledge. He then counters that acknowledgement by noting that not all people think prudence is knowledge and tries to escape the problem by bringing both virtue and knowledge under the genera of state and disposition. In other words, Aristotle concludes that insofar as it is a virtue, prudence is both a state and a disposition and thus somehow related to knowledge.

Averroes states Aristotle's original problem very clearly and then goes on to deny in his own name that practical virtue encompasses knowledge or is encompassed by it, i.e., that it is either a genus or species of knowledge. In this sense, he can be said to reject the notion that knowledge and practical virtue are the same. Holding his silence about Aristotle's mention of the disagreement between those who insist that prudence is subsumed by both practical virtue and knowledge as against those who deny it, Averroes tries to identify the logical problem involved here. He sees the problem as arising from the attempt to ascribe two genera to a single thing without first taking note of the hierarchy between the two genera. And then he asserts that this kind of problem very frequently occurs when inductive arguments are used. At this point, he proposes two solutions to the problem. If one wishes to insist that knowledge and virtue be subsumed under one genus, it is necessary to place them under the genus of disposition (malakah). Such a solution is in agreement with Aristotle's conclusion. However, in case the difficulties implicit in that solution appear too overwhelming, Averroes suggests an alternative solution — one which restates the position he set forth in his own name at the outset. Here, too, he speaks in the first person plural and notes that while knowledge is subsumed under theoretical virtue, it is not subsumed under practical virtue.(33)

Whatever the difficulties involved in this latter solution, the major point to be noted here is that Averroes emphatically traces the origin of the problem to faulty logical practice. He contends that the dilemma is logical rather than substantive and is due to a mistaken attempt to formulate arguments by means of induction. For him, correct logical procedure is grounded in the sound use of syllogism.

V

Averroes's conviction about the relationship between the syllogism and correct logical procedure finds its fullest expression in the third part of his commentary. Although he is willing to concede that the induction does have a place in the art of dialectic, he considers its role subservient to that of the syllogism and brings forth a number of different arguments to elucidate the importance of the syllogism in all dialectical discussion. Before commenting on Aristotle's enumeration of the four ways in which the answerer can prevent the

questioner from arriving at his conclusion, Averroes formulates an argument of his own to show why only a questioner who bases his questions on induction is open to these kinds of objections. Explaining that the generally accepted opinions expressed in a proposition based on an induction can be perceived only after intermediate explanations, he insists that the four refutations or criticisms by Aristotle are directed to precisely these kinds of explanations. Moreover, throughout his prefatory remarks, Averroes carefully states why all of these inductive propositions must ultimately be expressed as premises of syllogisms.(34)

He is persuaded that those interested in dialectic must distrust the induction insofar as it is based on sense-perceived objects and on particulars. Anyone trying to draw similarities between these objects will frequently be subject to the kinds of errors which befall all arguments based on sense perceptions. Such a liability to error far outweighs any advantage the induction might otherwise offer because of its immediate appeal to the common people. Although it is possible to make a mistake when constructing a syllogism, Averroes does not consider that possibility as likely an occurrence as that of being led into error due to the faulty formulation or use of the induction. Throughout this last part of the commentary, instances of the way the induction can lead astray abound. Moreover, there is a strong suggestion that it is an especially useful device for those interested in sophistical tricks.(35)

Averroes is so intent upon restricting the role of the induction in dialectic that he does not hesitate to deny it the virtues ascribed by Aristotle. Thus, despite his earlier acknowledgement of Aristotle's argument about its value for getting at the first principles of the arts and sciences and promise to explain this value later, Averroes never returns to that subject. If anything, his references to the sophistical arguments based on inductions used by those who claim to get at the first principles of the arts or sciences deny that virtue. And when he does identify the kinds of first principles one might discuss in dialectic, he claims that they are reached by means of syllogisms or definitions. He is completely silent about the possible role played by the induction in reaching these kinds of first principles.(36) Conversely, Averroes attaches far greater importance to the decorative role for which the dialectical induction can be used. Devoting two pages to a subject Aristotle deems worthy of two lines,

Averroes explains how the induction can be used either to draw out a dialectical argument through the enumeration of particular examples or to render it more appealing. Because he considers such pursuits similar to the use of agreeable colors in clothes and houses to create a feeling of restfulness or to induce pleasure, he thinks they have a secondary kind of usefulness in dialectic. Yet even though he admits at the end of this long digression that the induction used for this purpose differs from the one used for substantive purposes in dialectic, his comments on its more serious use are far more limited in scope. When discussing the way the induction may be used to establish the necessary premise in dialectic or to make it firmer, he explains the way it is used in such a succinct manner as to make it appear that the induction has a role in dialectical argument only by default. Finally, Averroes's silence about Aristotle's attempt to distinguish a questionable manner of drawing similarities between things from the correct uses of induction to draw similarities suggests that such a distinction is either invalid or unimportant.(37)

When commenting on Aristotle's explanation of how the questioner may meet the objections an answerer might bring against an argument he has established by induction, Averroes misrepresents Aristotle's remarks in such a way as to weaken the force of the induction. According to Aristotle, if the questioner has used an induction to establish a universal premise and the answerer refuses to assent to it, he is perfectly justified in demanding why the answerer has refused to assent. Averroes thinks the answerer is obliged to state his reasons for refusing to assent to such a premise only if he has already assented to all the particulars enumerated as part of the induction. If the answerer has simply refused the induction, then it falls upon the questioner to divine what part of the induction might be objectionable.(38) In other words, Averroes sees the induction as so eminently open to objection that he places stronger restrictions on its use than Aristotle.

Part of the reasoning behind the low esteem in which Averroes holds the induction is set forth in a series of proportions he draws between the technical devices used in the different logical arts. Presented as an explanation of the fourth goal for which the external or non-necessary premises are used, Averroes's account has no parallel in Aristotle's text. He begins by asserting that this fourth goal, rendering the argument clearer or explaining it, is carried out either

by means of examples or by means of illustrations and imaginative representations. While all three serve this purpose in the art of dialectic, illustrations and imaginative representations can also be used for the same purpose in the art of demonstration. He says nothing here about the way examples might be used in that art. He does note, however, that each of these devices can be used for another purpose in logical arts and that in this respect they have something in common with the way the induction is used in this art. Examples are used in rhetoric in the same way that illustrations and imaginative representations are used in poetics and that the induction is used in dialectic: in each art, these devices are used to bring about assent (tagdīy). And Averroes also explains that the induction is used in demonstration for the sake of drawing examples.(39)

Clearly there are a number of problems with Averroes's account of these technical arts. Above all, he has said nothing about the technical device used to bring about a concept (tagawwur) in any of these logical arts — namely, the syllogism, the enthymeme, or the speeches which take the place of the syllogism in poetics. If bringing about a concept is the major goal of logical discourse, then bringing about assent and making something comprehensible are secondary and tertiary goals. But there is another problem with this account. While Averroes's failure to say anything about the syllogism seems to explain his silence concerning what brings about a concept in any of these arts, it does not explain why he says nothing about what brings about assent in demonstration. The only clear implication is that none of these devices performs that function.

Averroes's attempt to compare the way these devices are used in the different logical arts also points to an important hierarchy between the arts and the devices. Each one can be used to bring about assent only in the lower of the two arts in which it is used. That is to say, the induction which is used in demonstration and in dialectic brings about assent only in dialectic; likewise, the examples used in dialectic and rhetoric bring about assent only in the latter art; and the case is even more pronounced when the illustrations and imaginative representations used in demonstration, dialectic, and poetics are considered. Moreover, the induction is presented here as having something in common with these lesser technical devices and nothing in common with the syllogism. Like them, it can be used for bringing about assent; nothing is said of its possible use for bringing about

a concept. And this implied negative judgment is so strong that it cannot be adequately balanced by the addition of implication that the induction is apparently too noble a device to be used for making something comprehensible. It still remains less noble than the syllogism.

Were it to be objected that this is too harsh a conclusion because the induction does provide a link between the art of demonstration and the art of dialectic, it would be sufficient to draw attention to the fact that illustrations and imaginative representations provide a firmer link between the two arts. After all, they alone are said to perform the same function in each art. That they are also used for this function in the art of poetics does not detract from their merit, for Averroes says nothing here against the art of poetics. Even though its inclusion as a logical art is tenuous, it is not as questionable an art as rhetoric or sophistry. These two are always singled out by Averroes because of the way logical arguments can be used in them to achieve whatever goal the practitioner desires. Neither art has an internal standard which would rule out speech directed to base ends. And when Averroes emphasizes the closeness between dialectic and demonstration at the beginning of this third part of his commentary, he does so by noting that they are similar insofar as particular attention is paid in each art to making the argument clearer or more understandable. (40) In other words, it is not the induction which brings these two arts together, but the illustrations and imaginative representations used in both of them as well as in poetics.

Now to speak of similarities and links between these two arts is to acknowledge that they are distinct. Averroes does not deny such a distinction; he simply blurs its edges or renders it less sharply than Aristotle. The major reason given throughout this commentary for the two arts being distinct is the way the syllogism is used in each. It is such an important consideration for Averroes that he returns to it here on at least two different occasions. In both instances, he freely adapts Aristotle's text to serve his own purpose.

At the very beginning of the third part of his commentary, Averroes explains that the difference between the dialectician and the philosopher whom he identifies as one who uses demonstrative syllogisms — centers around the different way they use the syllogism. Because the dialectical syllogism is always used between a questioner and answerer, the dialectician must pay attention to the proper for-

mulation of questions and answers. But since the philosopher uses the demonstrative syllogism only with himself, he need not concern himself about anything other than the soundness of his premises. (41) The demonstrative man or the philosopher carries on an internal discussion and has to persuade no one but himself. The dialectician cannot achieve his goal unless, as questioner, he can bring the answerer to assent to a proposition which overturns his original thesis or unless, as answerer, he can keep the questioner from bringing him to such an admission.

Another way in which dialectic may be distinguished from demonstration centers around the use of the different kinds of syllogisms in each. While both the ostensive syllogism and the hypothetical syllogism per impossible can be used without hesitation in demonstration, the use of the latter syllogism leads to problems in dialectic. Whereas Aristotle makes the problem with this syllogism turn on the difficulty of getting someone else to admit to the contradiction or to the impossible thesis in dialectic, Averroes omits that explanation and argues instead that the contradictory or contested part of dialectical argument must be cast in terms which go against what is generally accepted. As a result, it would be repulsive (shamī) and thus should not be uttered. He sees no such problem with respect to the fallacy (kādhīb) brought out by means of this kind of syllogism in demonstration. (42)

Here, as elsewhere in the commentary, Averroes speaks as though dialectic were addressed to the many rather than to just another person. Differently stated, by paying such attention to the conventional character of dialectical speech, Averroes suggests that it is primarily an art used in addressing the many. At one point he transforms Aristotle's suggestion that the dialectician should have arguments ready for frequently occurring issues into a rule that the dialectician must concern himself with the arguments familiar to the many. (43) In his eyes, dialectic is above all a useful art for instructing the populace. However, as has already been noted, that is not its only usefulness. Here, as in the beginning of this commentary, Averroes speaks as though the arguments used in dialectic and in the various sciences are the same. Despite his explicit acknowledgements of a distinction between dialectic and demonstration, he insists on linking the two arts. Thus, when Aristotle explains that the good dialectician must seek to become proficient in the art of dialectic,

Averroes agrees and then identifies the goal of such proficiency as that of attaining demonstrative knowledge. (4) Averroes's dialectician is nothing if he is not a philosophe-apprenti — a philosophic-apprenti primarily interested in the political uses of his art.

VI

If nothing else, the above remarks prove the importance of a close, comparative reading of Averroes's commentary with Aristotle's text. But they do more. It should now be clear that Averroes views demonstration as the central logical art. All the other logical arts are judged in terms of their proximity to it, and elsewhere all of the logical tools are presented as leading up to demonstrative reasoning. It should also be clear that Averroes views the art of dialectic as uniquely similar to demonstration. Finally, it should be clear that Averroes attaches unusual political importance to dialectic in this commentary. As it is presented here, dialectic rather than rhetoric is the appropriate logical art to be used for instructing the multitude.

By reading the two texts closely, it has been possible to gain a clearer image of how Averroes functions as a commentator. Many passages attest to this concern about presenting Aristotle's argument clearly. Nonetheless, he sees no reason why such a concern should force him to repeat blithely everything Aristotle utters. Instead, he chooses to emphasize some points and to omit others. He substitutes his own examples for Aristotle's on occasion and sometimes simply omits Aristotle's examples. Usually, he does so in order to make Aristotle's argument clearer. At times, however, he does so in order to strengthen his own argument — an argument that is as much at variance with the Arabic translation of Aristotle now available to us as with our version of Aristotle's Greek text. And one point must never be forgotten: Averroes explicitly and repeatedly warns the reader that he has selectively commented on Aristotle's text; he never promises his reader a verbatim repetition of Aristotle's argument.

Unfortunately, the most important question must go unanswered. An answer to that question — why Averroes chooses to present this selective reading of Aristotle's text — must await further study of his other commentaries on logic and further study of the other works in which he discusses dialectic. But at this point, it might not be too temerarious to suggest an answer along the following lines. Aver-

roes modifies Aristotle's teaching in a manner that makes it more closely resemble Plato's because of the importance he attaches to syllogistic argument. And he sees the possibility of drawing closer parallels between the premises used in dialectic and those used in demonstration because he thinks that the opinions of the learned do resemble the truth. Those are the opinions he would modify in order to instruct the many about the things they need to believe so as to associate in justice and virtue.

This edition of Averroes's Middle Commentary on Aristotle's Topics is based on the two Arabic manuscripts of the text which are now extant. One of these manuscripts is located in the Laurentian library in Florence and the other in the library of the University of Leiden. Both are very well-preserved and written in a clear Maghribi script. Although neither manuscript is dated, the ownership of the Leiden manuscript can be traced to the latter part of the sixteenth century. The bibliographic history of the Florence manuscript shows that it was in Europe as early as the beginning of the seventeenth century, but a note on the first page of the manuscript identifies as owners of the manuscript individuals known to have lived in North Africa in the eighth century, A.H., i.e., the fourteenth century, C.E.

The Florence manuscript is classified as CLXXX, 54 in the Codice Orientale Laurenziano of the Biblioteca Medicea Laurenziana. It consists of 208 folios bound together in twenty quires of 10 folios, plus an additional quire containing 8 folios. Although the first two folios are numbered on each side of the page, the numbering comes out correctly in the end because folios 11 and 127 have been numbered twice. We have indicated the beginning of each page of the manuscript in the margins of the edited text. The pages measure 28.5 cm. in length and 20.5 cm. in width, with the writing occupying 21.5 cm. of the length and 14 cm. of the width. Each page contains thirty-five lines of writing.

This manuscript contains all of Averroes's Middle Commentaries on Aristotle's logical works, i.e., his Middle Commentaries on the Categories, De Interpretatione, Prior Analytics, Posterior Analytics, Topics, Sophistics, Rhetoric, and Poetics. The Middle Commentary on Aristotle's Topics takes up 38 folios of the collection. It starts on folio 88, recto, and ends on folio 125, recto. Two dates occur in the Florence manuscript, one at the end of the second part of this commentary and the other at the end of the commentary on the Rhetoric. According to the first, Averroes finished writing the second part of the commentary on the Topics in the month of Rajab, 563 A.H., i.e., in the month of April, 1168 C.E. And the second explains

that he finished writing the commentary on the Rhetoric in the month of Muharram, 571 A.H., i.e., in the month of July, 1175 C.E.

The Leiden manuscript is classified in the Codicium Orientalium of the University of Leiden library as no. 2073. It contains 231 folios bound together in twenty-three quires of 10 folios, plus one additional folio which seems to have been added later. The Arabic scribe, at any rate, says at the very end of the volume that it only contains 230 folios. But there are other problems with the numbering of the pages. Folios 20 and 107 have been numbered twice, and the very first folio has not been numbered. Thus the last folio of the volume is numbered as 228 rather than 230 or 231. Moreover, folios 118, verso, to 127, verso, are numbered at the lower right-hand side of the page and written upside down, suggesting that this quire of the volume was turned upside down at one point. We have indicated the beginning of each page of the manuscript in the margins of the edited text and distinguished between the two folios numbered as 107 by referring to them as 107a and 107b. The pages are 25.5 cm. long and 18 cm. wide, and the writing occupies 21.5 cm. by 13.3 cm. of the page. There are 31 lines to each page.

Various titles and inscriptions on the first page of the Leiden manuscript show that it once belonged to Jesuits in Paris as well as to the orientalist Guillaume Postel and that it was at one time in the possession of Jewish scholars. Throughout the volume, there are also numerous notes and titles written in Greek.

Like the Florence manuscript, this too contains all of Averroes's Middle Commentaries on Aristotle's logical works. His Middle Commentary on Aristotle's Topics takes up 41 folios. It starts on folio 102, verso, and ends on folio 143, recto. Although no date is to be found in this manuscript at the end of the second part of the commentary on the Topics, one does occur at the end of the commentary on the Rhetoric. Unlike the corresponding date in the Florence manuscript, this reference speaks of the work being completed in the month of Sha'ban, 570 A.H., i.e., in the month of February, 1175 C.E., or about six months earlier than the Florence manuscript.

This difference in dating was one of the major reasons for choosing the Florence manuscript as the base manuscript in this edition. The difference in date suggests that it represents a version revised by Averroes subsequent to composing the version represented in the Leiden manuscript. It thus takes on the status of an author's

revised edition. And such an interpretation of the relationship between the two manuscripts is especially helpful for explaining some of the different readings found in them. On a number of occasions, the student of the text can discern attempts by Averroes to make his argument clearer and his reasoning tighter. The Florence manuscript also offers, generally speaking, better stylistic variants than the Leiden manuscript.

The paragraphing found in this edition has been added. It tries to follow lines suggested by Averroes — as when he breaks his argument and explains that this is what Aristotle said (cāl) or that he himself has something to say (naqūl, qulnā, aqūl) — or to reflect the general structure of his commentary and of Aristotle's treatise. Every effort has been made to keep punctuation to a minimum, the goal being to reproduce an understandable text that is not too far from the one Averroes originally composed. His references to his own writings or to other books by Aristotle have been identified whenever possible. And the relationship between his commentary and Aristotle's text has been indicated by placing the corresponding passages of Aristotle's text, numbered according to the Bekker edition (Berlin, 1813), in the margins of the edited text.

C.E.B.

September, 1978

صفحة

كتاب الجدل لأرسطو ... ... ... ... ... ... ... ... ... ... ٢٥٤

هذا الكتاب الذي تقدمه - وهو تلخيص كتاب الجدل - يعد خامس

الكتب في النشرة العلمية لكتاب تلخيص كتب أرسطو في المنطق لأبي الوليد ابن رشد . أما الكتاب الأربع الأولي - وهي تلخيص كتاب المقولات

وتلخيص كتاب العبارة وتلخيص كتاب الفياس وتلخيص كتاب البرهان - فقد

تأثر صدورها للأسباب التي نبينها بعد . وقد أشرنا في العنوان إلى كتاب الجدل بأنه الكتاب السادس ، لأن لابن رشد تلخيصاً لكتاب إيساغوجي يسبق

هذه الكتاب وهو كالمدخل فما ، ولأنعرف له مخطوطة عربية إلى الآن ، وقد

نشرت ترجمته العبرية ١٩٦٩ م .

والغرض من هذه النشرة هو إيجاز العمل الطموح الذي بدأه الأستاذ المرحوم

الدكتور محمود محمد قاسم عميد كلية دار العلوم بجامعة القاهرة ورئيس قسم

الفلسفة بها سابقاً . وكان المرحوم الدكتور محمود محمد قاسم قد بدأ في هذا العمل

قبل سنوات قليلة من وفاته واستمر يعمل به إلى أن اختطفته منا يد المTON ،

وكان إكتمالاً للعمل لحجة من الوفاء والتقدير لكري الفقيد الذي نكنه له شخصياً

ويساركتنا فيه زملاؤه وطلابه الماولون في حقل الدراسات الفلسفية الإسلامية .

يمكن لهذا المشروع أن يبدأ، ثم مساعدات معهد سميسونيان والجمعية الفلسفية الأمريكية في إكمال هذا المشروع. ولا بد من تقديم شكري للسيد همام فوزى حسن الباحث بمركز تحقيق التراث بالهيئة المصرية العامة للكتاب بالقاهرة لما قدمه من عون مثمر. وأخيراً أود أن أعبر عن خالص شكري للاستاذ أحمد عبد الحميد هريدى الذي شارك بعلمه الواسع في اللغة العربية وخبرته الطويلة في دراسة المخطوطات في تحقيق نص غريب عن حقل اختصاصه ووفق بين معرفته بالقديم والحديث من مستويات التحقيق العلمى فكان خير زميل في العمل.

تشارلس بترورث

القاهرة في ٩ سبتمبر ١٩٧٨

ولظروف خارجية عن إرادةنا ميصدر هذا الكتاب في غير الترتيب الطبيعي لصدوره. أحدها أننا أنشأه قياماً بإعداد الكتاب الأربعة الأولى للطبعة - وهي الكتاب التي كان المرحوم الدكتور محمود قاسم قد أرشك على الانتهاء من إعدادها - وفقنا إلى المثور على مخطوطات كانت جمهولة من قبل. وذلك نتيجة صدور فهارس جديدة لمخطوطات بعض المكتبات، ونتيجة لإرشاد الإملاء والرفاق في البحث، وأيضاً نتيجة لبحث في القوائم الغير منشورة لصيد المخطوطات في بعض المكتبات. وكانت بيانات بعض هذه المخطوطات توجد تحت عناوين أخرى منسوبة لغير ابن رشد. ثم إن هذه المخطوطات في نخزائن الكتاب في المعد وإيران وإنجلترا وإيرلندا وبعضها في نخزائن خاصة من العسير الحصول على مصوّرات منها. وقد نجحنا في الحصول على أكثرها ومازلنا في انتظار ما تبقى منها. وقد فضلنا أن يصدر كتاب الجدل الآن إذ لا تعرف له مخطوطات غير التي بين أيدينا. ونأمل أن تصدر الكتاب الأربعة الأولى محققة قريباً إن شاء الله.

وأود في هذا التصدير أن أتوه بالتشجيع الأدبي والعون والتوجيه الذي قدمه لهذا المشروع الأستاذ الدكتور محسن مهدي وبدوره الرائد في النهضة في الدراسات الفلسفية الإسلامية. كما يجب أن أذكر المساعدات المادية والأدبية التي قدمها مركز البحوث الأمريكي ب Emerson ولزملائي بفضل رعاية وتوجيه مديرة الأستاذ الدكتور بول ووكر. وعلى أن أضيف أيضاً تقديري وشكري للعاونة التي قدمتها مؤسسة فولبرايت للأبحاث بالولايات المتحدة الأمريكية والتي لولاها ما كان

منهج التحقيق

اعتمدنا في تحقيقنا لهذا الكتاب على النسختين الخطيتين التي تختلف بواحداها مكتبة لورنزيا بمدينة فلورنزا بإيطاليا وبالأنخرى مكتبة جامعة ليدن بهولندا. وكل واحدة منهما في حالة جيدة وكتبت بخط مغربي واضح. ولا يوجد فيها ما يجدد تاريخ كتابتهما، ولكن عند الفحص في المللكات الموجودة على الصفحة الأولى من مخطوطة ليدن أمكن تحديد وجودها بأوربا في نهاية القرن السادس عشر الميلادي. وأما نسخة فلورنزا فقد ذكر في فهرس المكتبة أنها وردت إلى أوربا في أول القرن السابع عشر الميلادي، إلا أن بعد البحث في كتب التراجم عن سيرة بعض الذين تملكوا المخطوط اتضح لنا أنها كانت موجودة بالمغرب في القرن الثامن الهجري أي القرن الرابع عشر الميلادي.

وخطوطة فلورنزا رقها 54، CLXXX، وعدد أوراقها ٢٠٨ ورقة، ورقت أولا الصفحات من ١ – ٤، ثم بعد ذلك رققت الأوراق. وقد أشرنا إلى الأوراق في هاش النسخة المطبوعة. وقد تكرر الرقم ١١ على ورقتين وكذلك الرقم ١٢٧. وعدد كراريس المخطوطة ٢١ كراسة كل كراسة في ١٠ أوراق مما الأأخيرة وفيها ٨ أوراق. وعدد سطور الصفحة ٣٥ سطرًا. وتحتوي المخطوطة على تلخيص للكتب الثمانية لأرسطو في المنطق. ويسدأ تأخيص كتاب الجدل في الورقة ٨٨ وينتهي في الورقة ١٢٥، أي أنه يقع في حوالي ٣٨ ورقة.

شيعان سنة ٧٠. من الهجرة المقابل لشهر فبراير سنة ١١٧٥ من الميلاد أي قبل حوالي نصف عام من التاريخ المذكور في خطوطه فلورزا . ومن هذا يظهر أن الأصل الذي نقلت عنه نسخة ليدن يمثل في أغلب الظن التحرير الأول لتخيص كتب أرسطو في المنطق وأن الأصل الذي تقلت عنه نسخة فلورزا يمثل تحريزا ثانيا قام به ابن رشد نفسه . فلذلك اعتمدنا خطوطه فلورزا أصلا للتحقيق . فهي تتمثل بصورة أحدث وأوضح لفكر ابن رشد كما أنها كتبت بعبارة أسلم وأقوم .

وقد قسمنا النص إلى فقرات وحاولنا أن تكون كل فقرة دالة على قول أرسطو حين يذكر ابن رشد كلامه « قال » أو أن تكون دالة على قول ابن رشد حين يذكر كلامه « فلنا » أو كلامه « أقول » . وحاولنا حين أغفل ابن رشد الإشارة إلى قول أرسطو أو إلى قول هو أن تكون الفقرات مطابقة للترتيب العام الذي يسلكه أرسطو في كتابه . وقد أشرنا في الهامش إلى أرقام صفحات وسطور نص أرسطو حسب نشرة بيكر لكتب أرسطو

( برلين ١٨٣١ م ) .

وفي الخطوط تاريخان، أحدهما في آخر الجُزء الثاني من تلخيص كتاب الجدل، والثاني في آخر الخطابة . ومن التاريخ الأول تستطيع أن نعرف أن ابن رشد انتهى من الجُزء الثاني من تلخيصه لكتاب الجدل في شهر رجب سنة ٦٣٥ من الهجرة أي شهر أبريل سنة ١١٦٨ من الميلاد . ومن التاريخ الثاني تستطيع أن نعرف أنه انتهى من تلخيصه لكتاب الخطابة في شهر محرم سنة ٧١٥ من الهجرة أي شهر يوليو سنة ١١٧٥ من الميلاد . وأما خطوطه ليدن فرقها ٧٣ . وعدد أوراقها ٣٣ . ورقة ، إلا أن ترقيمها يشير إلى أن عدد الأوراق ٢٢٨ ورقة، وهذا ناتج عن تكرار رقم الورقة ٢٠ ورقم الورقة ١٠٧ . وقد أشرنا إلى أرقام الأوراق في هامش النسخة المطبوعة، وأشرنا إلى هذا التكرار بالأرقام ١١٠٧ و ١١٧٠ ب . ويلاحظ أن الكراسة التي تختوي على الأوراق ١١٨ إلى ١٢٧ رقت حديثا بعد أن كانت قد وضعت مقلوبة عند ورود الخطوط إلى المكتبة، وكان ترقيمها الأصل ١٢٧ إلى ١١٨ . وعدد كراريس الخطوط ٣٣ كراسة كل كراسة في ١٠ أوراق . ودل الترتيب على عدد أوراق الخطوط بأن كتب « رل » في آخرها ويساوي بحسب الجُمل ٣٣٠ . وتوجد ورقة زائدة في أول الخطوط كتب عليها عنوان الكتاب باللغة العربية واليونانية وتملكها باللغة اللاتينية والفرنسية . وعدد مطOLOR الصفحه ٣١ سطرا . وتحتوي الخطوط على تلخيص لكتاب الثانية لأرسطو في المنطق ويبدا تلخيص كتاب الجدل في الورقة ١٠٢ ظ ويتبعه في الورقة ٤٣ و ٤٤ أي أنه يقع في ٤١ ورقة . وفي الخطوط تاريخ واحد فقط وهو التاريخ الذي يوجد في آخر تلخيص كتاب الخطابة، ونستطيع أن نعرف منه أن ابن رشد انتهى من تلخيصه لكتاب الخطابة في أصل هذه النسخة في شهر

رموز الكتاب

ف : خطوط رقمه 54, CLXXX, في مكتبة لورتريانا بمدينة فلورنزا

بإيطاليا.

ل : خطوط رقمه ٢٠٧٣ في مكتبة جامعة ليدن بهولندا.

ه : إهمال في النقط.

ح : في الحاشية.

يداً : ما كتبته يد غير يد ناسخ الخطوط.

+ : زيادة.

— : نقص.

< > : ليس في الخطوطتين وتفترح إضافته.

[ ] : في الخطوطتين وتفترح حذفه.
